<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Yttrium is a soft, silver-metallic, lustrous and highly crystalline <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Group_3_element" title="Group 3 element">group 3</a>. As expected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_trend" title="Periodic trend">periodic trends</a>, it is less <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegative</a> than its predecessor in the group, <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, and less electronegative than the next member of <a href="/wiki/Period_5_element" title="Period 5 element">period 5</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>; additionally, it is more electronegative to its successor in its group, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>, being closer in electronegativity to the later <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> due to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide contraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p946_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p946-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond-10">[10]</a></sup> Yttrium is the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-block" title="D-block">d-block</a> element in the fifth period.
</p><p>The pure element is relatively stable in air in bulk form, due to <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a> of a protective oxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) film that forms on the surface. This film can reach a thickness of 10 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">µm</a> when yttrium is heated to 750 °<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a> in <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup> When finely divided, however, yttrium is very unstable in air; shavings or <a href="/wiki/Swarf" title="Swarf">turnings</a> of the metal can ignite in air at temperatures exceeding 400 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_nitride" title="Yttrium nitride">Yttrium nitride</a> (YN) is formed when the metal is heated to 1000 °C in <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similarity_to_the_lanthanides">Similarity to the lanthanides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Similarity to the lanthanides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_element" title="Rare-earth element">Rare-earth element</a></div>
<p>The similarities of yttrium to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a> are so strong that the element has historically been grouped with them as a <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_element" title="Rare-earth element">rare-earth element</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC1-4">[4]</a></sup> and is always found in nature together with them in <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_mineral" title="Rare-earth mineral">rare-earth minerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley498_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley498-12">[12]</a></sup> Chemically, yttrium resembles those elements more closely than its neighbor in the periodic table, <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE810_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE810-13">[13]</a></sup> and if physical properties were plotted against <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, it would have an apparent number of 64.5 to 67.5, placing it between the lanthanides <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE815_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE815-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>It often also falls in the same range for reaction order,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup> resembling <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dysprosium" title="Dysprosium">dysprosium</a> in its chemical reactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup> Yttrium is so close in size to the so-called 'yttrium group' of heavy lanthanide ions that in solution, it behaves as if it were one of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p945_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p945-15">[15]</a></sup> Even though the lanthanides are one row farther down the periodic table than yttrium, the similarity in atomic radius may be attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide contraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p1234_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p1234-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the few notable differences between the chemistry of yttrium and that of the lanthanides is that yttrium is almost exclusively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valency_(chemistry)" title="Valency (chemistry)">trivalent</a>, whereas about half the lanthanides can have valences other than three; nevertheless, only for four of the fifteen lanthanides are these other valences important in aqueous solution (<a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">Ce<sup>IV</sup></a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarium" title="Samarium">Sm<sup>II</sup></a>, <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">Eu<sup>II</sup></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">Yb<sup>II</sup></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds_and_reactions">Compounds and reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Compounds and reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Yttrium_compounds" title="Category:Yttrium compounds">Category:Yttrium compounds</a>.</div>
<p>As a trivalent transition metal, yttrium forms various <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">inorganic compounds</a>, generally in the oxidation state of +3, by giving up all three of its <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p948_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p948-17">[17]</a></sup> A good example is <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_oxide" title="Yttrium(III) oxide">yttrium(III) oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), also known as yttria, a six-<a href="/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond" title="Coordinate covalent bond">coordinate</a> white solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p947_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p947-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium forms a water-insoluble <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a>, but its <a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">bromide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> are all <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup> The Y<sup>3+</sup> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a> is colorless in solution because of the absence of electrons in the d and f <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">electron shells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a> readily reacts with yttrium and its compounds to form <span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley498_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley498-12">[12]</a></sup> Concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acids</a> do not rapidly attack yttrium, but other strong acids do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>With <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, yttrium forms <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">trihalides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_fluoride" title="Yttrium(III) fluoride">yttrium(III) fluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">YF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_chloride" title="Yttrium(III) chloride">yttrium(III) chloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">YCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_bromide" title="Yttrium(III) bromide">yttrium(III) bromide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">YBr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) at temperatures above roughly 200 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> all form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_compound" title="Binary compound">binary compounds</a> with yttrium at elevated temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Organoyttrium_chemistry" title="Organoyttrium chemistry">Organoyttrium chemistry</a> is the study of compounds containing carbon–yttrium bonds. A few of these are known to have yttrium in the oxidation state 0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloke1993_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cloke1993-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schumann_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schumann-20">[20]</a></sup> (The +2 state has been observed in chloride melts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikheev1992_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mikheev1992-21">[21]</a></sup> and +1 in oxide clusters in the gas phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kang2005_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kang2005-22">[22]</a></sup>) Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trimerization" title="Trimerization">trimerization</a> reactions were generated with organoyttrium compounds as catalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schumann_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schumann-20">[20]</a></sup> These syntheses use <span class="chemf nowrap">YCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> as a starting material, obtained from <span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> and concentrated <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hapticity" title="Hapticity">Hapticity</a> is a term to describe the coordination of a group of contiguous atoms of a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> bound to the central atom; it is indicated by the Greek character <i>eta</i>, η. Yttrium complexes were the first examples of complexes where <a href="/wiki/Carborane" title="Carborane">carboranyl</a> ligands were bound to a d<sup>0</sup>-metal center through a η<sup>7</sup>-hapticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schumann_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schumann-20">[20]</a></sup> Vaporization of the <a href="/wiki/Graphite_intercalation_compound" title="Graphite intercalation compound">graphite intercalation compounds</a> graphite–Y or graphite–<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> leads to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Endohedral_fullerene" title="Endohedral fullerene">endohedral fullerenes</a> such as Y@C<sub>82</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_spin_resonance" title="Electron spin resonance">Electron spin resonance</a> studies indicated the formation of Y<sup>3+</sup> and (C<sub>82</sub>)<sup>3−</sup> ion pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a> Y<sub>3</sub>C, Y<sub>2</sub>C, and YC<sub>2</sub> can be hydrolyzed to form <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE817_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-ECE817-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes_and_nucleosynthesis">Isotopes and nucleosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes and nucleosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_yttrium" title="Isotopes of yttrium">Isotopes of yttrium</a></div>
<p>Yttrium in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> was created through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, mostly by the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> (≈72%), but also by the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> (≈28%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pack_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pack-25">[25]</a></sup> The r-process consists of rapid <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> of lighter elements during <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions. The s-process is a slow <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> capture of lighter elements inside pulsating <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p12-13_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p12-13-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mira_1997.jpg"><img alt="Grainy irregular shaped yellow spot with red rim on a black background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="350" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mira_1997.jpg/220px-Mira_1997.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mira_1997.jpg/330px-Mira_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/Mira_1997.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mira_1997.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mira" title="Mira">Mira</a> is an example of the type of red giant star where most of the yttrium in the solar system was created</div></div></div>
<p>Yttrium isotopes are among the most common products of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> of uranium in nuclear explosions and nuclear reactors. In the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a> management, the most important isotopes of yttrium are <sup>91</sup>Y and <sup>90</sup>Y, with half-lives of 58.51 days and 64 hours, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> Though <sup>90</sup>Y has a short half-life, it exists in <a href="/wiki/Secular_equilibrium" title="Secular equilibrium">secular equilibrium</a> with its long-lived parent isotope, <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a> (<sup>90</sup>Sr) with a half-life of 29 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>All group 3 elements have an odd <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, and therefore few stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p946_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p946-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">Scandium</a> has one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable isotope</a>, and yttrium itself has only one stable isotope, <sup>89</sup>Y, is also the only isotope that occurs naturally. However, the lanthanide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earths" title="Rare earths">rare earths</a> contain elements of even atomic number and many stable isotopes. Yttrium-89 is thought to be more abundant than it otherwise would be, due in part to the s-process, which allows enough time for isotopes created by other processes to decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">electron emission</a> (neutron → proton).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p12-13_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p12-13-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[note 1]</a></sup> Such a slow process tends to favor isotopes with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_number" title="Atomic mass number">atomic mass numbers</a> (A = protons + neutrons) around 90, 138 and 208, which have unusually stable <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> with 50, 82, and 126 neutrons, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p12-13_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p12-13-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> <sup>89</sup>Y has a mass number close to 90 and has 50 neutrons in its nucleus.
</p><p>At least 32 synthetic isotopes of yttrium have been observed, and these range in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_number" title="Atomic mass number">atomic mass number</a> from 76 to 108.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> The least stable of these is <sup>106</sup>Y with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of &gt;150 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefix" title="SI prefix">ns</a> (<sup>76</sup>Y has a half-life of &gt;200 ns) and the most stable is <sup>88</sup>Y with a half-life of 106.626 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> Apart from the isotopes <sup>91</sup>Y, <sup>87</sup>Y, and <sup>90</sup>Y, with half-lives of 58.51 days, 79.8 hours, and 64 hours, respectively, all the other isotopes have half-lives of less than a day and most of less than an hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium isotopes with mass numbers at or below 88 decay primarily by <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a> (proton → neutron) to form <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Z</a> = 38) isotopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> Yttrium isotopes with mass numbers at or above 90 decay primarily by electron emission (neutron → proton) to form <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> (Z = 40) isotopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> Isotopes with mass numbers at or above 97 are also known to have minor decay paths of β<sup>−</sup> delayed <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">neutron emission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium has at least 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable_isomer" title="Metastable isomer">metastable ("excited") isomers</a> ranging in mass number from 78 to 102.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[note 3]</a></sup> Multiple excitation states have been observed for <sup>80</sup>Y and <sup>97</sup>Y.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NNDC_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-NNDC-27">[27]</a></sup> While most of yttrium's isomers are expected to be less stable than their <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>, <sup>78m</sup>Y, <sup>84m</sup>Y, <sup>85m</sup>Y, <sup>96m</sup>Y, <sup>98m1</sup>Y, <sup>100m</sup>Y, and <sup>102m</sup>Y have longer half-lives than their ground states, as these isomers decay by beta decay rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomeric_transition" title="Isomeric transition">isomeric transition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1787, army lieutenant and part-time chemist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Axel_Arrhenius" title="Carl Axel Arrhenius">Carl Axel Arrhenius</a> found a heavy black rock in an old quarry near the Swedish village of <a href="/wiki/Ytterby" title="Ytterby">Ytterby</a> (now part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stockholm_Archipelago" title="Stockholm Archipelago">Stockholm Archipelago</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krogt_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krogt-5">[5]</a></sup> Thinking that it was an unknown mineral containing the newly discovered element <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley496_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley496-32">[29]</a></sup> he named it <i>ytterbite</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[note 4]</a></sup> and sent samples to various chemists for analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krogt_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Krogt-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johan_Gadolin.jpg"><img alt="Black and white bust painting of a young man with neckerchief in a coat. The hair is only faintly painted and looks grey." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="422" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johan_Gadolin.jpg/150px-Johan_Gadolin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johan_Gadolin.jpg/225px-Johan_Gadolin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Johan_Gadolin.jpg/300px-Johan_Gadolin.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johan_Gadolin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johan_Gadolin" title="Johan Gadolin">Johan Gadolin</a> discovered yttrium oxide</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johan_Gadolin" title="Johan Gadolin">Johan Gadolin</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_%C3%85bo" title="University of Åbo">University of Åbo</a> identified a new oxide (or "<a href="/wiki/Earth_(chemistry)" title="Earth (chemistry)">earth</a>") in Arrhenius' sample in 1789, and published his completed analysis in 1794.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[note 5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anders_Gustaf_Ekeberg" title="Anders Gustaf Ekeberg">Anders Gustaf Ekeberg</a> confirmed the identification in 1797 and named the new oxide <i>yttria</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p944_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p944-36">[31]</a></sup> In the decades after <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> developed the first modern definition of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a>, it was believed that earths could be reduced to their elements, meaning that the discovery of a new earth was equivalent to the discovery of the element within, which in this case would have been <i>yttrium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[note 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1843, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gustaf_Mosander" title="Carl Gustaf Mosander">Carl Gustaf Mosander</a> found that samples of yttria contained three oxides: white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_oxide" title="Yttrium oxide">yttrium oxide</a> (yttria), yellow <a href="/wiki/Terbium(III,IV)_oxide" title="Terbium(III,IV) oxide">terbium oxide</a> (confusingly, this was called 'erbia' at the time) and rose-colored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erbium_oxide" title="Erbium oxide">erbium oxide</a> (called 'terbia' at the time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[32]</a></sup> A fourth oxide, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ytterbium_oxide" title="Ytterbium oxide">ytterbium oxide</a>, was isolated in 1878 by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Charles_Galissard_de_Marignac" title="Jean Charles Galissard de Marignac">Jean Charles Galissard de Marignac</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[33]</a></sup> New elements were later isolated from each of those oxides, and each element was named, in some fashion, after Ytterby, the village near the quarry where they were found (see <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">ytterbium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">terbium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka115_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka115-40">[34]</a></sup> In the following decades, seven other new metals were discovered in "Gadolin's yttria".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krogt_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Krogt-5">[5]</a></sup> Since yttria was found to be a mineral and not an oxide, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a> renamed it <a href="/wiki/Gadolinite" title="Gadolinite">gadolinite</a> in honor of Gadolin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krogt_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Krogt-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> mistakenly thought he had isolated the metal in 1828 from a volatile chloride he supposed to be yttrium chloride,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[36]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rose" title="Heinrich Rose">Heinrich Rose</a> proved otherwise in 1843 and correctly isolated the element himself that year.
</p><p>Until the early 1920s, the chemical symbol <b>Yt</b> was used for the element, after which <b>Y</b> came into common use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1987, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_barium_copper_oxide" title="Yttrium barium copper oxide">yttrium barium copper oxide</a> was found to achieve <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductivity" title="High-temperature superconductivity">high-temperature superconductivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-44">[38]</a></sup> It was only the second material known to exhibit this property,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-44">[38]</a></sup> and it was the first known material to achieve <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a> above the (economically important) boiling point of nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg"><img alt="Three column shaped brown crystals on a white background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="655" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg/200px-Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg/300px-Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg/400px-Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenot%C3%ADmio1.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Xenotime" title="Xenotime">Xenotime</a> crystals contain yttrium</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abundance">Abundance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Abundance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Yttrium is found in most <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_mineral" title="Rare-earth mineral">rare-earth minerals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond-10">[10]</a></sup> it is found in some <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> ores, but is never found in the Earth's crust as a free element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup> About 31 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> of the Earth's crust is yttrium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup> making it the 28th most abundant element, 400 times more common than <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup> Yttrium is found in soil in concentrations between 10 and 150 ppm (dry weight average of 23 ppm) and in sea water at 9 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_trillion" title="Parts per trillion">ppt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup> Lunar rock samples collected during the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Project" title="Apollo Project">Apollo Project</a> have a relatively high content of yttrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka115_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka115-40">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium has no known biological role, though it is found in most, if not all, organisms and tends to concentrate in the liver, kidney, spleen, lungs, and bones of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[41]</a></sup> Normally, as little as 0.5 milligrams is found in the entire human body; human <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">breast milk</a> contains 4 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley495_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley495-49">[42]</a></sup> Yttrium can be found in edible plants in concentrations between 20 ppm and 100 ppm (fresh weight), with <a href="/wiki/Cabbage" title="Cabbage">cabbage</a> having the largest amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley495_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley495-49">[42]</a></sup> With as much as 700 ppm, the seeds of woody plants have the highest known concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley495_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley495-49">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  April 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there are reports of the discovery of very large reserves of rare-earth elements on a tiny Japanese island. <a href="/wiki/Minami-Tori-shima" title="Minami-Tori-shima">Minami-Torishima Island</a>, also known as Marcus Island, is described as having "tremendous potential" for rare-earth elements and yttrium (REY), according to a study published in Scientific Reports.
</p><p>"This REY-rich mud has great potential as a rare-earth metal resource because of the enormous amount available and its advantageous mineralogical features," the study reads. The study shows that more than 16 million tons of rare-earth elements could be "exploited in the near future."
</p><p>Including ytrrium (Y), which is used in products like camera lenses and mobile phone screens, the rare-earth elements found are: Europium (EU), Terbium (Tb) and Dysprosium (Dy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since yttrium is chemically so similar to the lanthanides, it occurs in the same ores (<a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_mineral" title="Rare-earth mineral">rare-earth minerals</a>) and is extracted by the same refinement processes. A slight distinction is recognized between the light (LREE) and the heavy rare-earth elements (HREE), but the distinction is not perfect. Yttrium is concentrated in the HREE group because of its ion size, though it has a lower <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanazawaa_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kanazawaa-52">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yttrium_1.jpg"><img alt="Roughly cube shaped piece of dirty grey metal with an uneven superficial structure." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="384" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Yttrium_1.jpg/200px-Yttrium_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Yttrium_1.jpg/300px-Yttrium_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Yttrium_1.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yttrium_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A piece of yttrium. Yttrium is difficult to separate from other rare-earth elements.</div></div></div>
<p>Rare-earth elements (REEs) come mainly from four sources:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-53">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Carbonate and fluoride containing ores such as the LREE <a href="/wiki/Bastn%C3%A4site" title="Bastnäsite">bastnäsite</a> ([(Ce, La, etc.)(CO<sub>3</sub>)F]) contain an average of 0.1%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup> of yttrium compared to the 99.9% for the 16 other REEs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup> The main source for bastnäsite from the 1960s to the 1990s was the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Pass_rare_earth_mine" title="Mountain Pass rare earth mine">Mountain Pass rare earth mine</a> in California, making the United States the largest producer of REEs during that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-53">[46]</a></sup> The name "bastnäsite" is actually a group name, and the Levinson suffix is used in the correct mineral names, e.g., bästnasite-(Y) has Y as a prevailing element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">Monazite</a> ([(<a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">Ce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">La</a>, etc.)<a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">PO<sub>4</sub></a>]), which is mostly phosphate, is a <a href="/wiki/Placer_deposit" title="Placer deposit">placer deposit</a> of sand created by the transportation and gravitational separation of eroded granite. Monazite as a LREE ore contains 2%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup> (or 3%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka116_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka116-57">[50]</a></sup> yttrium. The largest deposits were found in India and Brazil in the early 20th century, making those two countries the largest producers of yttrium in the first half of that century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-53">[46]</a></sup> Of the monazite group, the Ce-dominant member, monazite-(Ce), is the most common one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xenotime" title="Xenotime">Xenotime</a>, a REE phosphate, is the main HREE ore containing as much as 60% yttrium as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_phosphate" title="Yttrium phosphate">yttrium phosphate</a> (YPO<sub>4</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup> This applies to xenotime-(Y).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup> The largest mine is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayan_Obo" title="Bayan Obo">Bayan Obo</a> deposit in China, making China the largest exporter for HREE since the closure of the Mountain Pass mine in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-7"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-53">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ion absorption clays or Lognan clays are the weathering products of granite and contain only 1% of REEs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-8"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup> The final ore concentrate can contain as much as 8% yttrium. Ion absorption clays are mostly in southern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morteani_51-9"><a href="#cite_note-Morteani-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-53">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[55]</a></sup> Yttrium is also found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samarskite" title="Samarskite">samarskite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fergusonite" title="Fergusonite">fergusonite</a> (which also stand for group names).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>One method for obtaining pure yttrium from the mixed oxide ores is to dissolve the oxide in <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> and fractionate it by <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchange</a> <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a>. With the addition of <a href="/wiki/Oxalic_acid" title="Oxalic acid">oxalic acid</a>, the yttrium oxalate precipitates. The oxalate is converted into the oxide by heating under oxygen. By reacting the resulting yttrium oxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_fluoride" title="Yttrium fluoride">yttrium fluoride</a> is obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-63">[56]</a></sup>  When quaternary ammonium salts are used as extractants, most yttrium will remain in the aqueous phase.  When the <a href="/wiki/Counterion" title="Counterion">counter-ion</a> is nitrate, the light lanthanides are removed, and when the counter-ion is thiocyanate, the heavy lanthanides are removed. In this way, yttrium salts of 99.999% purity are obtained. In the usual situation, where yttrium is in a mixture that is two-thirds heavy-lanthanide, yttrium should be removed as soon as possible to facilitate the separation of the remaining elements.
</p><p>Annual world production of yttrium oxide had reached 600 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> by 2001; by 2014 it had increased to 7,000 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-mcs15-Y_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-mcs15-Y-64">[57]</a></sup> Global reserves of yttrium oxide were estimated in 2014 to be more than 500,000 tons. The leading countries for these reserves included Australia, Brazil, China, India, and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-mcs15-Y_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-mcs15-Y-64">[57]</a></sup> Only a few tonnes of yttrium metal are produced each year by reducing <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_fluoride" title="Yttrium(III) fluoride">yttrium fluoride</a> to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_sponge" title="Metal sponge">metal sponge</a> with <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> alloy. The temperature of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arc_furnace" title="Arc furnace">arc furnace</a> of greater than 1,600 °C is sufficient to melt the yttrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-63">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer">Consumer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Consumer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aperture_Grille.jpg"><img alt="Forty columns of oval dots, 30 dots high. First red than green than blue. The columns of red starts with only four dots in red from the bottom becoming more with every column to the right" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="353" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Aperture_Grille.jpg/220px-Aperture_Grille.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Aperture_Grille.jpg/330px-Aperture_Grille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/Aperture_Grille.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aperture_Grille.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yttrium is one of the elements that was used to make the red color in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CRT_television" title="CRT television">CRT televisions</a></div></div></div>
<p>The red component of <a href="/wiki/Color_television" title="Color television">color television</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tubes</a> is typically emitted from an <a href="/wiki/Yttrium(III)_oxide" title="Yttrium(III) oxide">yttria (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>)</a> or yttrium oxide sulfide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>) host lattice <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">doped</a> with <a href="/wiki/Europium" title="Europium">europium (III)</a> cation (Eu<sup>3+</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[note 8]</a></sup> The red color itself is emitted from the europium while the yttrium collects energy from the <a href="/wiki/Electron_gun" title="Electron gun">electron gun</a> and passes it to the phosphor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE818_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE818-66">[58]</a></sup> Yttrium compounds can serve as host lattices for doping with different <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> cations. <a href="/wiki/Terbium" title="Terbium">Tb<sup>3+</sup></a> can be used as a doping agent to produce green <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">luminescence</a>. As such yttrium compounds such as yttrium aluminium garnet (YAG) are useful for phosphors and are an important component of white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LEDs</a>.
</p><p>Yttria is used as a <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintering</a> additive in the production of porous <a href="/wiki/Silicon_nitride" title="Silicon nitride">silicon nitride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[59]</a></sup> It is used as a common starting material for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Material_science" title="Material science">material science</a> and for producing other compounds of yttrium.
</p><p>Yttrium compounds are used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> As a metal, yttrium is used on the electrodes of some high-performance <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spark_plugs" title="Spark plugs">spark plugs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[60]</a></sup> Yttrium is used in <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantles</a> for <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> <a href="/wiki/Lantern" title="Lantern">lanterns</a> as a replacement for <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, which is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently under development is yttrium-stabilized zirconia as a solid electrolyte and as an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_sensor" title="Oxygen sensor">oxygen sensor</a> in automobile exhaust systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Garnets">Garnets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Garnets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yag-rod.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Yag-rod.jpg/220px-Yag-rod.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Yag-rod.jpg/330px-Yag-rod.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/Yag-rod.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yag-rod.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nd:YAG laser rod 0.5 cm in diameter</div></div></div>
<p>Yttrium is used in the production of a large variety of <a href="/wiki/Garnet#Synthetic_garnets" title="Garnet">synthetic garnets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup> and yttria is used to make <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_iron_garnet" title="Yttrium iron garnet">yttrium iron garnets</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span></span>, also "YIG"), which are very effective <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_filter" title="Electronic filter">filters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Yttrium, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a> garnets (e.g. Y<sub>3</sub>(Fe,Al)<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> and Y<sub>3</sub>(Fe,Ga)<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>) have important <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetic</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> YIG is also very efficient as an acoustic energy transmitter and transducer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_aluminium_garnet" title="Yttrium aluminium garnet">Yttrium aluminium garnet</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Y<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span></span> or YAG) has a <a href="/wiki/Mohs_scale_of_mineral_hardness" title="Mohs scale of mineral hardness">hardness</a> of 8.5 and is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstone</a> in jewelry (simulated <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">Cerium</a>-doped yttrium aluminium garnet (YAG:Ce) crystals are used as phosphors to make white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LEDs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YAG" title="YAG">YAG</a>, yttria, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_lithium_fluoride" title="Yttrium lithium fluoride">yttrium lithium fluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">LiYF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_orthovanadate" title="Yttrium orthovanadate">yttrium orthovanadate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">YVO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) are used in combination with <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">dopants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ytterbium" title="Ytterbium">ytterbium</a> in near-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cw_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-cw-75">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup> YAG lasers can operate at high power and are used for drilling and cutting metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka116_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka116-57">[50]</a></sup> The single crystals of doped YAG are normally produced by the <a href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">Czochralski process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Material_enhancer">Material enhancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Material enhancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Small amounts of yttrium (0.1 to 0.2%) have been used to reduce the grain sizes of <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup> Yttrium is used to increase the <a href="/wiki/Strength_of_materials" title="Strength of materials">strength</a> of aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> The addition of yttrium to alloys generally improves workability, adds resistance to high-temperature recrystallization, and significantly enhances resistance to high-temperature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> (see graphite nodule discussion below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE818_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ECE818-66">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium can be used to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxidizer" title="Deoxidizer">deoxidize</a> <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">non-ferrous metals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttria" title="Yttria">Yttria</a> stabilizes the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_zirconia" title="Cubic zirconia">cubic form of zirconia</a> in jewelry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yttrium has been studied as a nodulizer in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nodular_cast_iron" title="Nodular cast iron">ductile cast iron</a>, forming the <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> into compact nodules instead of flakes to increase <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductility</a> and fatigue resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Having a high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, yttrium oxide is used in some <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> to impart <a href="/wiki/Shock_(mechanics)" title="Shock (mechanics)">shock</a> resistance and low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC2008_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-CRC2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Those same properties make such glass useful in <a href="/wiki/Camera_lens" title="Camera lens">camera lenses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley497_47-5"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley497-47">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The radioactive isotope <a href="/wiki/Yttrium-90" title="Yttrium-90">yttrium-90</a> is used in drugs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_Y_90-DOTA-tyr3-octreotide" title="Yttrium Y 90-DOTA-tyr3-octreotide">Yttrium Y 90-DOTA-tyr3-octreotide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_Y_90_ibritumomab_tiuxetan" title="Yttrium Y 90 ibritumomab tiuxetan">Yttrium Y 90 ibritumomab tiuxetan</a> for the treatment of various <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Lymphoma" title="Lymphoma">lymphoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a>, liver, ovarian, colorectal, pancreatic and bone cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley495_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley495-49">[42]</a></sup> It works by adhering to <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibody" title="Monoclonal antibody">monoclonal antibodies</a>, which in turn bind to cancer cells and kill them via intense <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">β-radiation</a> from the yttrium-90 (see <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibody_therapy" title="Monoclonal antibody therapy">Monoclonal antibody therapy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>A technique called <a href="/wiki/Selective_internal_radiation_therapy" title="Selective internal radiation therapy">radioembolization</a> is used to treat <a href="/wiki/Hepatocellular_carcinoma" title="Hepatocellular carcinoma">hepatocellular carcinoma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metastatic_liver_disease" title="Metastatic liver disease">liver metastasis</a>. Radioembolization is a low toxicity, targeted liver cancer therapy that uses millions of tiny beads made of glass or resin containing radioactive yttrium-90. The radioactive microspheres are delivered directly to the blood vessels feeding specific liver tumors/segments or lobes. It is minimally invasive and patients can usually be discharged after a few hours. This procedure may not eliminate all tumors throughout the entire liver, but works on one segment or one lobe at a time and may require multiple procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also see Radioembolization in the case of combined cirrhosis and Hepatocellular carcinoma.
</p><p>Needles made of yttrium-90, which can cut more precisely than scalpels, have been used to sever pain-transmitting <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley496_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley496-32">[29]</a></sup> and yttrium-90 is also used to carry out radionuclide <a href="/wiki/Synovectomy" title="Synovectomy">synovectomy</a> in the treatment of inflamed joints, especially knees, in sufferers of conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>A neodymium-doped yttrium-aluminium-garnet laser has been used in an experimental, robot-assisted radical <a href="/wiki/Prostatectomy" title="Prostatectomy">prostatectomy</a> in canines in an attempt to reduce collateral nerve and tissue damage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> and erbium-doped lasers are coming into use for cosmetic skin resurfacing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotton_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cotton-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superconductors">Superconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Superconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductor" title="High-temperature superconductor">high-temperature superconductor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YBCO-modified.jpg"><img alt="Dark grey pills on a watchglass. One cubic piece of the same material on top of the pills." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="616" data-file-width="731" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/YBCO-modified.jpg/220px-YBCO-modified.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/YBCO-modified.jpg/330px-YBCO-modified.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/YBCO-modified.jpg/440px-YBCO-modified.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YBCO-modified.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBCO" title="YBCO">YBCO</a> superconductor</div></div></div>
<p>Yttrium is a key ingredient in the <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_barium_copper_oxide" title="Yttrium barium copper oxide">yttrium barium copper oxide</a> (YBa<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, aka 'YBCO' or '1-2-3') <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">superconductor</a> developed at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Houston" title="University of Houston">University of Houston</a> in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-44">[38]</a></sup> This superconductor is notable because the operating superconductivity temperature is above <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>'s boiling point (77.1 K).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-44">[38]</a></sup> Since liquid nitrogen is less expensive than the <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">liquid helium</a> required for metallic superconductors, the operating costs for applications would be less.
</p><p>The actual superconducting material is often written as YBa<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7–<i>d</i></sub>, where <i>d</i> must be less than 0.7 for superconductivity. The reason for this is still not clear, but it is known that the vacancies occur only in certain places in the crystal, the copper oxide planes, and chains, giving rise to a peculiar oxidation state of the copper atoms, which somehow leads to the superconducting behavior.
</p><p>The theory of low temperature superconductivity has been well understood since the <a href="/wiki/BCS_theory" title="BCS theory">BCS theory</a> of 1957. It is based on a peculiarity of the interaction between two electrons in a crystal lattice. However, the BCS theory does not explain high temperature superconductivity, and its precise mechanism is still a mystery. What is known is that the composition of the copper-oxide materials must be precisely controlled for superconductivity to occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>This superconductor is a black and green, multi-crystal, multi-phase mineral. Researchers are studying a class of materials known as <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskites</a> that are alternative combinations of these elements, hoping to develop a practical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-temperature_superconductor" title="High-temperature superconductor">high-temperature superconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka116_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka116-57">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yttrium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Yttrium currently has no biological role, and it can be highly <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a> to humans and other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water-soluble compounds of yttrium are considered mildly toxic, while its insoluble compounds are non-toxic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley495_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley495-49">[42]</a></sup> In experiments on animals, yttrium and its compounds caused lung and liver damage, though toxicity varies with different yttrium compounds. In rats, inhalation of yttrium citrate caused <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a>, while inhalation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_chloride" title="Yttrium chloride">yttrium chloride</a> caused liver edema, <a href="/wiki/Pleural_effusion" title="Pleural effusion">pleural effusions</a>, and pulmonary hyperemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to yttrium compounds in humans may cause lung disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup> Workers exposed to airborne yttrium europium vanadate dust experienced mild eye, skin, and upper respiratory tract irritation—though this may be caused by the <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a> content rather than the yttrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup> Acute exposure to yttrium compounds can cause shortness of breath, coughing, chest pain, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanosis" title="Cyanosis">cyanosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">limits</a> exposure to yttrium in the workplace to 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) is 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. At levels of 500 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, yttrium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Yttrium dust is flammable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osha_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-osha-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
